>
>## 1. Overall Evaluation and Recommendation of the Manuscript
>
>- **Overall Evaluation**

## 翻译 private_upload\default_user\2025-01-26-14-53-12\README.md.part-150.md

> This manuscript addresses the issue of nonlinear frequency response drift in electrochemical seismometers (MET) under different amplitudes by proposing a method that integrates frequency response information into a Kolmogorov–Arnold network (FRIKAN) for compensation. The approach demonstrates significant innovation and engineering application value. The article is well-structured, with a systematic experimental design, and the choice of comparison methods (WIENER, GRU, LSTM, RVTDCNN, etc.) is representative.
> The highlights of this paper are:
>
> 1. The injection of the physical prior "relative frequency response" into the RNN kernel, combined with the KAN using trainable B-spline activation functions, achieves a "white-box" compensation framework for MET nonlinearity;
> 2. The LUT acceleration strategy achieves good real-time performance on embedded platforms;
> 3. The proposed amplitude-frequency response loss function (AFMAE) effectively avoids overfitting to high-frequency noise, demonstrating practical utility.
>
> Overall, the article presents novel ideas, clear logic, and convincing experimental results, largely meeting the submission requirements of **IEEE TIE** in terms of industrial application and theoretical innovation. Further revisions and improvements on the following issues will significantly enhance the persuasiveness and readability of the paper.
>
>- **Recommendation:**
>   Given that the manuscript still requires further clarification and supplementation in several areas, **it is recommended to undergo a "Major Revision" before resubmission**. If the authors can adequately address the following revision comments and provide the necessary supplementary experiments or data, the manuscript has a good chance of being accepted.
>
>------
>
>## 2. Detailed Reasons for Revision or Supplementation (Sorted by Severity)
>
> The following comments, listed from most to least critical, highlight several areas requiring revision or supplementation for the authors' reference.
>
>### (1) Further Explanation on Model Universality and Physical Constraints
>
>- Severity: High
>
>   Although the authors discuss the interpretability based on the Kolmogorov–Arnold theorem, there is a lack of systematic explanation regarding "why this method is applicable in more general nonlinear sensor compensation scenarios." It is recommended that the authors supplement the introduction or methodology section with:
>
>  1. The extent to which the KAN structure can be applied to various nonlinear systems (not limited to electrochemical seismometers);
>  2. A discussion on the potential adaptability of the network, with the addition of B-spline activation functions, to other typical nonlinear sensors (e.g., optical, ultrasonic sensors);
>  3. Whether additional priors (e.g., impedance measurement, temperature compensation) are needed to extend the method to other applications.
>
>### (2) Insufficient Comparative Experiments and Sensitivity Analysis
>
>- Severity: Medium-High
>
>   Although the paper compares multiple models (WIENER, GRU, LSTM, RVTDCNN, etc.), there is a lack of in-depth discussion on hyperparameter sensitivity and the criticality of the amplitude-frequency response loss weight 
>
>  \wMAE\wMAE\wMAE
>
>  values. It is recommended to:

## 翻译 private_upload\default_user\2025-01-26-14-53-12\README.md.part-151.md

> 1. Add an "Ablation Study" or "Sensitivity Analysis," for example, compare the compensation effects when \wMAE=0\wMAE = 0\wMAE=0, \wMAE=0.2\wMAE = 0.2\wMAE=0.2, \wMAE=0.5\wMAE = 0.5\wMAE=0.5, etc.;
> 2. Compare the compensation accuracy and model size changes with different B-spline orders (0,1,2,3) and grid numbers (e.g., 6, 8, 16);
> 3. If possible, add stability test results under different initial frequencies or temperature conditions to demonstrate the model's generalization ability.
>
>### (3) Detailed Explanation of the Look-Up Table (LUT) Acceleration Strategy
>
>- Severity: Medium
>
>  The paper proposes an LUT (Look-Up Table) acceleration method and compares the inference time on STM32F405. However, more detailed experiments or explanations are needed regarding the LUT resolution and accuracy, as well as whether additional interpolation errors are introduced under different interpolation methods. For example:
>
>  - If the LUT sampling points are increased to 2000 or more, how does the inference time change?
>  - Is this solution still feasible when the flash memory resources of the embedded system are very limited? Can the LUT size be flexibly adjusted according to requirements?
>  - Has piecewise linear interpolation or other simplified interpolation methods been considered to ensure good accuracy even with small LUT sizes?
>
>### (4) Insufficient Association with Existing "Predistortion" or "Compensation" Methods
>
>- Severity: Medium
>
>  The author briefly mentions "Digital Predistortion" (DPD) methods commonly used in power amplifier linearization in the introduction. However, there is a lack of more specific comparative discussion on how to borrow DPD ideas to construct a sensor post-compensator. It is recommended to supplement:
>
>  - What are the similarities and differences between FRIKAN and conventional DPD in network implementation or parameter calibration?
>  - Is it possible to replace the FRIRNN kernel with existing DPD's orthogonal components or polynomial structures? What would be the pros and cons of doing so?
>  - Can compensation based on DPD ideas achieve the same nonlinear suppression effect?
>
>### (5) Readability and Consistency Issues with Some Symbols and Formulas
>
>- Severity: Medium
>
>  The paper contains a large number of formulas (e.g., 7, 8, 9, etc.), and some symbols (
>
>  \Sen\Sen\Sen
>
>  , 
>
>  η\etaη
>
>  , 
>
>  ζ\zetaζ
>
>    etc.) have slightly different meanings in different sections, which may require frequent backtracking by readers.
>
>  Recommendations:
>
>  1. Add a clear symbol table or "Notation" section to uniformly list the main symbols, meanings, and units;
>  2. Add appropriate spacing or line breaks between formulas to ensure aesthetic typesetting.
>
>### (6) Visualization of Experimental Data and Multilingual Labeling
>
>- Severity: Medium or Low

> The current figures contain a mix of Chinese and English annotations. Some figures (e.g., Figure 3, Figure 5) use Chinese explanations, but for formal publication in English journals, it is usually necessary to unify them into English or provide bilingual annotations. Additionally, the resolution and font size of some images can be further optimized to better fit IEEE TIE formatting.

> Suggestions:
>
> - Use English titles and annotations for all figures and tables uniformly in the revised manuscript;
> - Improve image resolution to ensure that lines and text remain clear after scaling;
> - Differentiate curve colors or use a consistent legend layout in the figures.
>
>### (7) Completeness of References and Addition of New Literature
>
>- Severity: Medium or Low
>
>  The authors are advised to consider supplementing the latest achievements from the past two to three years in the fields of sensor nonlinear compensation or physics-informed fusion networks, such as:
>
>  - Recent physics-inspired deep learning methods for sensor nonlinear compensation;
>  - More advancements on B-spline activation functions or trainable activation functions;
>  - Recent works in IEEE TIE related to PINNs/Hybrid Physics ML, etc.
>
>------
>
>## 3. Conclusion on the Presence of Structural Fatal Flaws
>
>From this reviewer's perspective, **the manuscript does not exhibit obvious "structural fatal flaws."** Its overall approach, theoretical derivations, and experimental design are systematic, and the methodological innovations are relatively sufficient. As long as the aforementioned revisions are completed and necessary comparative experiments and discussions are supplemented, it is believed that potential reader concerns can be effectively addressed.
>
>------
>
>## 4. Whether the Paper Lacks Key Data/Figures That Could Lead to Rejection
>
>The data and figures in the paper are relatively comprehensive, including experimental comparisons under multiple frequencies and amplitudes, time-domain and frequency-domain metrics, and inference time comparisons for embedded implementations. No absolutely critical data is missing. The only point for the authors to note is:
>
>- If "parameter sensitivity" or "ablation test" supplementary figures can be added, it would help reviewers better understand the dependency of FRIKAN on hyperparameters and loss function design;
>- Additionally, some source data tables, such as a more detailed comparison table of LUT points vs. storage space vs. inference accuracy, could be included in the appendix or supplementary materials to further demonstrate engineering feasibility.
>
>If the above suggestions are well addressed in the revised manuscript, no additional experimental data will be needed to support the conclusions, and there should be no risk of rejection.
>
>------
>
>### Comprehensive Suggestions
>
>In summary, this manuscript demonstrates good innovation and academic value, making it suitable for publication in **IEEE Transactions on Industrial Electronics**. A **Major Revision** is recommended, focusing on improving the explanation of physical constraint principles, enriching comparative experiments, and enhancing discussions on the connections with literature and predistortion methods. Further improvements in figure formatting and language consistency are also advised. If the authors can adequately address these points and revise accordingly, this reviewer is inclined to **recommend acceptance** in subsequent rounds. Looking forward to the revised manuscript.

![image-20250120104533678](assets/image-20250120104533678.png)

WaveNet variants are prone to gradient vanishing

```python
        for dilation in dilations:
            skip_connections = []
            for _ in range(3):  # Three residual blocks for each dilation
                # Multi-scale convolutions with different kernel sizes
                x_res1 = tf.keras.layers.Conv1D(kernel_units, kernel_size, dilation_rate=dilation, padding='causal')(x)
                x_res2 = tf.keras.layers.Conv1D(kernel_units, kernel_size*2, dilation_rate=dilation, padding='causal')(x)
                x_res3 = tf.keras.layers.Conv1D(kernel_units, kernel_size//2, dilation_rate=dilation, padding='causal')(x)
                skip_connections.append(x_res1)
                skip_connections.append(x_res2)
                skip_connections.append(x_res3)
            x = tf.keras.layers.Add()(skip_connections)
            x = tf.keras.layers.Conv1D(kernel_units, kernel_size, padding='causal')(x)

        x = tf.keras.layers.Dense(kernel_units, activation=activation)(x)
        x = tf.keras.layers.Dense(1, activation='linear')(x)

```

It might be because no activation function is added after the conv layers, making it difficult to train.
